@sebastian_byron True, the app-based notifications are a great way to keep people informed. Not sure how it funds the news companies but it might be a good start.
@sebastian_byron I think you're right, though if they click through to those stories and hit a paywall - they likely go back to scrolling the newsfeed. The consequence is that readers are less informed and publications don't get any clicks. There's got to be a solution in there somewhere.
I'll finish this thread by looping back to my first tweet - check out @JackCraver's article on the State of the @Statesman /end	  https://t.co/ArCJll2jdI
Is this news industry in part hamstrung by outdated business models as much as it is by new economic landscapes? I don't know, but it's an important dialogue for us to have. /7
One of the problems real news has competing with "fake news" is that fake news is free while real news is parked behind paywalls. I realize these papers need to exist, but also realize that most consumers can't/won't pay for subscriptions to more than one or two papers. /6
In a world where @netflix, @Spotify, and @AppleMusic have carved out niches in their various sectors, why can't there be a similar subscription service for news that isn't limited to one publication? /5
The non-profit route seems to be a viable option for media, specifically online- only productions that emphasize social and video. But there are limits here, too, as most markets will reach saturation among donors at some point. Which leads me to my main point... /4
Some news entities have found a way to adapt, stay solvent, and still provide a service - most notably @evanasmith's successful @TexasTribune. But we can also look at @NPR and @KUT as models that have worked for a while now. /3
The for-profit model of news is not viable anymore. And not just due to a lack of advertisers, but also due to the high expense associated with operations. But this reality has hit other businesses as well, in particular retail. /2
I just finished reading @JackCraver's excellent piece on the state of the @statesman, which presents a gratuitous opportunity for me to share my own thoughts on the state of the media. Here's a short thread... /1

Ultimately, this isn't an ideological argument, it's a values argument - and values are what most Americans will get behind. /end
Here's the thing - moderation is dead because no one wants a candidate who waters down all of their positions to try and win votes from all people, they'll get votes from nowhere. We need to re-think moderation in terms of pragmatism - how can we fight for what we believe in.
He suggests we look at the moderates who won in 2018, which I agree is a good case study. Many of them ran on access to healthcare and public education and guess what - those are considered progressive issues. It is possible for something to be progressive and mainstream.
McAuliffe writes about the risk: "the stakes are another four years of Trump degrading our country." I agree, but the Governor's prescription appears to be for Dems to tell people what can't be done instead of telling people what we believe in. That's also a risk.
We can take the words of James Carville, "Don't complicate the simple," and update it to "don't complicate the message."
McAullife's call for a "more realistic alternative sounds like Mondale's "I'll raise your taxes" speech. Most voters don't have time to keep up with specifics of legislation, but they know what they believe and they want someone to fight for it.
I agree w/ McAuliffe that we can't simply play negative but do need to draw and contrast. In other words: when they go low, we go high and low.
I'm normally a fan of @TerryMcAuliffe, but his latest Op-Ed essentially states that Democrats need to ditch values messaging and embrace realistic messaging. Which is a perfect example of why Democrats need help with all messaging. https://t.co/nBRk7Pqg96
@elreyalto What I'm getting at here is that too often people are using the term "biweekly" to refer to things that happen every two weeks. That's not what biweekly means!
For 2019 can we agree that "biweekly" means twice per week and not every two weeks?

Age is a better predictor of voting behavior than national origin, @pbump, so the question shouldn't be "Did you move to Texas?" but rather "WHEN did you move to Texas?" Every other piece of data suggests you'll get a starkly different result. /end https://t.co/8sSlfw5anf
People moving to Texas are mostly younger (under 50) and almost entirely heading to the urban areas. Both young voters and urban areas are blue and getting bluer. The suburbs are also moving towards Democrats. /3
An 80-year-old voter who moved to Texas in the 1960's could be included in the "not from Texas" group, but it would also not be surprising for that person to vote for Cruz. Age, ethnicity, and the urban/rural split are better predictors of someone's voting preferences. /2
